<leme no="268">
<page sig="[*1r]">
<section type="Titlepage">
<heading>
THE 
Workes of our Anti&shy;
ent and lerned English Poet,
GEFFREY CHAVCER,
newly Printed.
</heading>

In this Impression you shall find
these Additions:

1 His Portraiture and Progenie shewed.

2 His Life collected.

3 Arguments to euery Booke gathered.

4 Old and obscure Words explaned.

5 Authors by him cited, declared.

6 Difficulties opened.

7 Two Bookes of his neuer before printed.


<term lang="la">LONDINI.

Impensis Geor. Bishop:
Anno. 1598.</term>
</section>
</page>

<page sig="[*1v]">
</page>

<page sig="[*2r]">
<section type="Dedication to Sir Robert Cecil">
<heading>
TO THE RIGHT HONORABLE 
SIR ROBERT CECIL KNIGHT, PRINCIPLE SECRE&shy;
TARIE TO THE QVEENS MOST EXCELLENT 
MAIESTIE, CHAVNCELLOR OF THE DVCHIE OF LANCASTER. 
AND ONE OF HIR HIGHNES MOST HONOV&shy;
RABLE PRIVIE COVNSEL.
</heading>

This book when it was first published 
in print (right Honorable) was de&shy;
dicated to the Kings most excellent 
Maiestie Henry the eight, who fa&shy;
uourably entertained the same, as a 
work deseruing a worthy patrone. 
These collections and corrections
vpon Chaucer as they earnestly de&shy;
sire, so might they better haue de&shy;
serued acceptance at your honors hands, had they ben as fully 
perfected, as they haue beene painefully gathered. But what is 
now wanting through lacke of time, may happily hereafter be 
supplied. I trust your Honor, for the Poets sake, so much alwais 
liked of the learned, and commended by the best, will receiue 
withall this already done in the Poets behalfe, till longer time 
and further search giue me better instructions. My dutifull re&shy;
membrance of that honourable good Lady your mother, who 
gaue mee yearely exhibition all the time of my continuance in 
Cambridge, enforceth me to offer this slender present vnto 
your Honour, as a testimonie of the dutie, wherein I acknow&shy;
ledge my selfe bound both to her and hers during my life. And 
so crauing your honorable acceptance of the same, and patro&shy;
nage of my good meaning towards the Poet, I humbly take 
my leaue, beseeching God long to continue your Ho&shy;
nour in health and happinesse.

Your Honors in all duty at 
commandement, 

Tho. Speght.
</section>
</page>

<page sig="[*2v]">
<section type="To the Readers">
<heading>
To the Readers.
</heading>

Some few yeers past, I was requested by certaine 
Gentlemen my neere friends, who loued Chau&shy;
cer, as he well deserueth; to take a little pains in 
reuiuing the memorie of so rare a man, as also in 
doing some reparations on his works, which they 
iudged to be much decaied by iniurie of time, ig&shy;
norance of writers, and negligence of Printers. For whose sakes 
thus much was then by me vndertaken, although neuer as yet fully 
finished: First, His life collected. Secondly, The text by old written 
Copies corrected. Thirdly, Arguments to euery booke prefixed. 
Fourthly, Old words explaned. Fiftly, Difficulties opened. Sixtly, 
Authors by him cited, declared. Seuenthly, Sentences noted. Eightly, 
Some things of his added which neuer had bene printed. As that 
little which then was done, was done for those priuat friends, so 
was it neuer my mind that it should be published. But so it fell out 
of late, that Chaucers Works being in the Presse, and three parts 
thereof alreadie printed, not only these friends did by their Letters 
sollicit me, but certaine also of the best in the Companie of Stationers 
hearing of these Collections, came vnto me, and for better or worse 
would haue something done in this Impression Whose importunitie 
hath caused me to commit three faults: first in publishing that which 
was neuer purposed nor perfected for open view: then, in putting di&shy;
urse things in the end of the booke, which els taken in time might 
haue bene bestowed in more fit place: lastly, in failing in some of 
those eight points, which might more fully haue bene performed, if 
warning and conuenient leisure had bene giuen. But seeing it is as 
it is, I earnestly entreat all friendly Readers, that if they find anie 
thing amisse they would lend me their skilfull helpe against some
</page>

<page sig="[*3r]">
other time, &amp; I wil thankefully receiue their labors, assuring them 
that if God permit, I wil accomplish whatsoeuer may be thought vn&shy;
perfect. And if herein I be preuented, those honest and learned Gen&shy;
tlemen that first set me on worke, haue promised to succeed mee in 
these my purposes. But howsoeuer it happen either in mine or their 
determination, I earnestly entreat al to accept these my endeuours in 
best part, as wel in regard of mine owne well meaning, as for the de&shy;
sert of our English Poet himselfe: who in most vnlearned times 
and greatest ignorance, being much esteemed, cannot in these our 
daies, wherein Learning and riper iudgement so much flourisheth,
but be had in great reuerence, vnlesse it bee of such as for want of 
wit and learning, were neuer yet able to iudge what wit or Learning 
meaneth. And so making no doubt of the friendly acceptance of 
such as haue taken pains in writing themselues, and hoping wel also 
of all others, that meane to employ any labour in reading, I 
commit our Poet to your fauourable affection, and 
your selues to the protection of the 
Almightie. 
</section>
</page>

<page sig="[*3v]">
<section type="Francis Beaumont to Thomas Speght">
<heading>
F.B. to his very louing friend, T.S.
</heading>

I Am sorrie that neither the worthinesse of Chaucers owne 
praise, nor the importunate praiers of diuerse your louing 
friendes can yet mooue you to put into print those good 
obseruations and collections you haue written of him. For 
as for the obiections, that in our priuate talke you are wont 
to say are commonly alledged against him, as first that ma&shy;
ny of his wordes (as it were with ouerlong lying) are growne too hard and vn&shy;
pleasant, and next that hee is somewhat too broad in some of his speeches, and 
that the worke therefore should be the lesse gratious: these are no causes, or no 
sufficient causes to withhold from Chaucer such desert of glorie, as at your 
pleasure you may bestow vpon him. For first to defend him against the first 
reproofe. 

It is well knowne to wise and learned men, that all languages be either such 
as are contained in learning, or such as be vsed amongst men in daily practise: 
and for the learned tongues, they hauing <term lang="la">Iure testamentario</term>, their legacies set 
downe by them that be dead, wordes must bee kept and continued in them in 
sort as they were left without alteration of the Testators wils in any thing. But 
for vsuall languages of common practise, which in choise of wordes are, and 
euer will bee subiect vnto chaunge, neuer standing at one stay, but sometimes 
casting away old wordes, sometimes renewing of them, and alwaies framing of 
new, no man can so write in them, as that lal his wordes may remaine currant 
many yeares. Which thing Horace rightly noteth, where hee saieth, that words 
in common tongues, like vnto fruites, must of necessitie haue their buddings, 
their blossomings, their ripenings, and their fallings: so that it was impossible 
that either Chaucer or any man liuing could keep th<expan type="e+_">em</expan> from falling after so long 
a time: And this hath happened amongst the LAtin writers themselues, when 
the Latine tongue was a spoken tongue, as ours now is: for diuers of <term lang="la">Statius, 
Ennius,</term> and <term lang="la">Plautus</term> haue beene long since by later Latinists reiected. 
But yet so pure were Chaucers wordes in his owne daies, as Lidgate that lear&shy;
ned man calleth him The Loadstarre of the English language: and so good they 
are in our daies, as Maister Spencer, following the counsaile of Tullie in de Orato&shy;
re, for reuiuing of antient wordes, hath adorned his own stile with that beauty 
and grauitie, which Tully speakes of: and his much frequenting of Chaucers 
antient speeches causeth many to allow farre better of him, then otherwise they 
would. And furthermore by your interpretation of the most vnusuall words, 
that hardnesse and difficultie is made most cleare and easie: and in the paines 
and diligence you haue vsed in collecting his life, mee thinkes you haue be&shy;
</page>

<page sig="[*4*r]">
stowed vpon him more fauorable graces then Medea did vpon Pelias: for you 
I haue restored vs Chaucer both aliue again and yong again, and deliuered many 
of the doubtfull coniectures they conceiued of him. And therefore though 
you haue not made euery thing perfect to your owne mind (for nothing at 
one time is both begun and perfected) yet since you haue opened the way 
to others, and attempted that which neuer was begun before you, your ende&shy;
uours herein cannot bee but very well accepted, vnlesse of such as are more 
readie to find fault, then willing to amend.

Touching the inciuilitie Chaucer is charged withall; What Romane Po&shy;
et hath lesse offended this way then hee? Virgil in his Priapus is worse by 
a thousand degrees, and Ouid in <term lang="la">de Arte amandi</term>, and Horace in manie pla&shy;
ces as deepe as the rest: but Catullus and Tibullus in vncleane wantonnesse 
beyond measure passe them all. Neither is Plautus nor Terence free in this 
behalfe: But these two last are excused aboue the rest, by their due obserua&shy;
tion of <term lang="la">Decorum</term>, in giuing to their comicall persons such manner of spee&shy;
ches as did best fit their dispositions. And may not the same bee saied for 
Chaucer? How much had hee swarued from Decorum, if hee had made his Mil&shy;
ler, his Cooke, and his Carpenter, to haue told such honest and good tales, as 
hee made his Knight, his Squire, his Lawyer, and his Scholler tell? But shew&shy;
ing the disposition of these meaner sort of men, hee declareth in their pro&shy;
logues and tales, that their chiefe delight was in vndecent speeches of their 
owne, and in their false defamations of others, as in these verses appea&shy;
reth:

<lemeformat>
<term lang="me">Let be thy leud dronken harlotry, 
It is a sinne and eke a great folly 
To apairen any man, or him defame, 
And eke to bring wiues in such blame.</term>
</lemeformat>

And a little after in excuse of himselfe for vttering those broad speeches of 
theirs, he vseth these words:

<lemeformat>
<term lang="me">Demeth not for Gods loue, that I say 
Of euill entent, but that I mote reherce 
Her tales all, ben they better of werce, 
Or els falsen some of my matere.</term>
</lemeformat>

So that no man can imagine in that large compasse of his, purposing 
to describe all men liuing in those daies, how it had beene possible for 
him to haue left vntouched these filthie delights of the baser sort of peo&shy;
ple.
And now to compare him with othe rPoets: His Caunterbury tales con&shy;
</page>

<page sig="[*4v]">
taine in them almost the same kind of Argument, that is handled in Come&shy;
dies: his Stile for the most part is lowe and like vnto theirs; but herein they 
differ: Terence followeth Plautus, Plautus Statius, Statius Menander, and 
Menander other Gr&aelig;cians before him. The ring they beate is this, and far&shy;
ther they goe not: to shewe the wantonnesse of some young women: the 
loosenesse of many young men: craftie schoole poynts of olde bawdes: 
the fawning flatterie of clawing Parasites: the miserie of diuers fonde fa&shy;
thers, who for sauing their money keepe their sonnes so long vnmarried, 
till in the ende they prouide some vnfortunate marches for themselues: and 
their notable follie in committing these children of theirs, to the attendance 
of their leudest and worst disposed seruing men. Chaucers deuise of his Can&shy;
terburie Pilgrimage is meerely his owne, without following the example of 
any that euer writ before him. His drift is to touch all sortes of men, and to 
discouer all vices of that age, and that he doth in such sort, as he neuer failes to 
hit euery marke he leuels at.

In his fiue Bookes of Troylus and Creside, and the BOoke of the praise 
of good women, and of the mercilesse Ladie, and that of Blaunch, and of 
his Dreame (which is in your handes and was neuer yet imprinted) hee soa&shy;
reth much higher then he did in the other before: and in his Troylus is so sen&shy;
tentious, as there bee fewe staues in that Booke, which are not concluded
with some principall sentence: most excellently imitating Homer and Virgil,
and borrowing often of them, and of Horace also, and other the rarest both
Oratours and Poets that haue written. Of whome, for the sweeetnesse of his
Poetrie may be saide, that which is reported of Stesichorus: and as Cethegus
was tearmed <term lang="la">Suade medulla</term>, so may Chaucer bee rightly called, The pith and 
sinewes of eloquence, and the verie life it selfe of all mirth and pleasant wri&shy;
ting: besides, one gifte hee hath aboue other Auctours, and that is, by the 
excellencie of his descriptions to possesse his Readers with a stronger
imagination of seeing that done before their eyes, which they reade, than
any other that euer writ in any tongue. And here I cannot forget to re&shy;
member vnto you those auncient learned men of our time in Cambridge,
whose diligence in reading of his workes them selues, and commending
them to others of the younger sorte, did first bring you and mee in loue
with him: and one of them at that time was and now is (as you knowe)
one of the rarest Schollers of the worlde. The same may bee saide of that
worthy man for learning, your good friend in Oxford, who with many o&shy;
ther of like excellent iudgement haue euer had Chaucer in most high repu&shy;
tation.

Now (M. Speght) tell mee, seeing not onely all Greeke and Latine Poets
haur had their interpretours, and the most of them translated into our
tongue,. but the French also and Italian, as Guillaume de Saluste seigneur du
Basrtas, that most diuine French poet, Petrarke, and Ariosto, of whome
this last instructed b7y M. Harrington doeth now speake as good English as
he did Italian before, and is withall encreased with many good notes, shall one&shy;
ly Chaucer our Poet, no lesse worthy than the best of them amongst all the Poets
of the world lie alwaies neglected and neuer be so well vnderstood of his owne
</page>

<page sig="[*5r]">
countriemen as strangers are? Well set your heart at rest, for seeing I was one
of them which first set you in hand with this worke, and since you haue giuen
me of your Copies to vse priuatly for mine owne pleasure, if you will not put
them forward your selfe, they shall abroad whether you will or no. Yet leest
many inconueniences might happen by this attempt of mine, and diuers
things are set foorth contraie vnto your owne liking, let mee once againe en&shy;
treat you (as I haue done often heretofore) to yeeld to my iust and reasonable
suit. Wherein you shall not onely satisfie that conceit which I haue many
yeares carried of your vnfained loue towards me: but pleasure many who day&shy;
expect your paines herein, and perfourme vnto Chaucer great part of 
that honour that he most worthely deserueth. So with my
thrise-heartie commendations I bed you farewel.
From Leicester the last of Iune,
<term lang="la">Anno.</term> 1597.

Your assured and euer louing friend,

Francis Beaumont.
</section>
</page>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">A moy, qui voy,</form> <xpln lang="en">To me which see.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">Bien moneste,</form> <xpln lang="en">Well admonished</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">Bien &amp; loialement,</form> <xpln lang="en">Well and dutifully.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">C'est sans dire, &amp;c.</form> <xpln lang="en">It is without saying, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">Dont vient la destinie,</form> <xpln lang="en">From whome commeth 
destinie.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">En dieu est,</form> <xpln lang="en">In God is.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">Entierement vostre,</form> <xpln lang="en">yours wholly.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">astreignes moy de c&oelig;ur ioyeux,</form> <xpln lang="en">Streine me with a
ioyfull heart.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">Et ie scay bien, que ce n'est pas mon tort,</form> <xpln lang="en">And I know
well that it is not my hurt.</xpln></wordentry>

in you.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">Ie vous dy,</form> <xpln lang="en">I say to you.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">Ie vous dy sans doute,</form> <xpln lang="en">I say to you without doubt.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">La belle dame sans mercy,</form> <xpln lang="en">The faire Lady without
mercy.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">L'ardant espoir en mon c&oelig;ur point est mort. D'auoir
l'amour de celle, que ie desire,</form> <xpln lang="en">The earnest hope
within my heart is not dead, to haue the loue
of her whome I desire.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">Onques puis leuer,</form> <xpln lang="en">I can neuer rise.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">Or &agrave; mon c&oelig;ur,</form> <xpln lang="en">Now to my heart.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">Or &agrave; mon c&oelig;ur ce qui vouloy,</form> <xpln lang="en">Now to my heart that
which I would.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">Pleures pour moy, s'il vou plaist amoreux,</form> <xpln lang="en">Weepe
for me if you please louely Lady.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">Plus ne pourroy,</form> <xpln lang="en">I can doe no more.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">Qui est la,</form> <xpln lang="en">Who is there.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">Qui bien aime, tard oublie,</form> <xpln lang="en">He that loueth well, is
slow to forget.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">Sans que iamais, &amp;c.</form> <xpln lang="en">Without euer, &amp;c.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="fr">Si douce est la marguerite,</form> <xpln lang="en">So sweet is the Daisie.</xpln></wordentry>

<section type="Chaucer's Authors">
<wordgroup1 type="a" lang="en"><alpha>Most of the Authours cited by G. Chaucer in his
workes, by name declared.</alpha>

<wordgroup2 type="a" object="A"><alpha>A</alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">ALhazen,</form> <xpln lang="en">an Arabian, wrote 7. bookes of 
Perspectiues.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Arnoldus de nova villa,</form> <xpln lang="en">did write the booke cal&shy;
led <term lang="la">Rosarium philosophorum</term></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Anselmus,</form> <xpln lang="en">Bishop of Canterburie, a great writer 
in Diuinitie, 1061.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Agathon,</form> <xpln lang="en">a Philosopher of Samos, did write Hi&shy;
stories.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Augustine,</form> <xpln lang="en">that famous Doctour and Bishoppe, wrote moe bookes then euer did any in the 
Church of the Latines.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Avicen,</form> <xpln lang="en">a Phisitian of Ciuill, wrote a multitude
of bookes.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Averroys,</form> <xpln lang="en">a Phisitian of Corduba <term lang="la">floruit</term>, 1149.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a" object="B"><alpha>B</alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Bernardus de Gordonio,</form> <xpln lang="en">a Frenchman borne, rea&shy;
der of Phisicke at Mount Pelier.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Barnardus Abbas Clarevallensis,</form> <xpln lang="en">a Burgonian and 
a singular diuine set forth many things, 1140.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Basilius Magnus,</form> <xpln lang="en">bishop of C&aelig;sarea, 367.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a" object="C"><alpha>C</alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Caer Iuda Zansis,</form> <xpln lang="en">and diuers other alledged by 
<term lang="la">Chaucer</term>, haue none or few of their workes
extant.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Claudianus,</form> <xpln lang="en">borne in Alexandria in Egypt, am<expan type="o+_">on</expan>g 
many things wrote a booke of the stealing 
away of <term lang="la">Proserpina.</term></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Crisippus</form> <xpln lang="en">did write a booke against the pleasure
of the bodie.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Constantine the Monke</form> <xpln lang="en">did translate and write
many things in Phisicke, and among other,
<term lang="la">Lib. de coitu, quibus modis augeatur &amp; diminua&shy;
tur.</term></xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a" object="D"><alpha>D</alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Dantes Aligerus,</form> <xpln lang="en">an Italian, and borne in Flo&shy;
rence, liued 1341.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Dares Phrigius,</form> <xpln lang="en">did write the Troian warre in 
Greeke where he himselfe was a souldier.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Ditis Historicus</form> <xpln lang="en">did write a booke of the Troian
warre, found in a certaine sepulchre.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Damascenus presbiter,</form> <xpln lang="en">did write many things in 
the Greeke tongue.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Dioscorides,</form> <xpln lang="en">a worshipfull knight of Egypt wrote 
in Greeke of the natures of diuers hearbes:
He liued vnder <term lang="la">Cleopatra</term> and <term lang="la">Antonius.</term></xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a" object="E"><alpha>E</alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">&AElig;sculapius</form> <xpln lang="en">did write a booke of the originall,
cause, and descriptions of diseases.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a" object="F"><alpha>F</alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Franciscus Petrarcha,</form> <xpln lang="en">an Italian borne, did write
when <term lang="la">Chaucer</term> was a young man. <term lang="la">floruit</term> 1374.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>

<wordgroup2 type="a" object="G"><alpha>G</alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Gatisden and Gilbertin,</form> <xpln lang="en">Englishmen borne, and
writers in Phisicke.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Guido de Columna,</form> <xpln lang="en">a Sicilian did write of the Tro&shy;
ian warre, 1287.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Galfride Vinesause</form> <xpln lang="en">was a Norman by his parents,
but borne in England: hee did write a Com&shy;
plaint for the death of Richard the first, who
was slain with an arow as he was in hunting.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Galenus,</form> <xpln lang="en">a most singular Phisitian, did write a 
multitude of bookes, 160.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup2>
</page>

<page sig="Bbbb2v">
<wordgroup2 type="a" object="H"><alpha>H</alpha>
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Hali</form> <xpln lang="en">wrote a booke of the Composition of me&shy;

<section type="Annotations and Corrections: General Prologue">
<wordgroup1 type="b" lang="en"><alpha>Corrections of some faults, and Annotations
vpon some places.</alpha>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">AND Palmers to seeken strange stronds)</form> <xpln lang="en">A Pilgrime and a Palmer did differ thus: The 
Pilgrime had some dwelling place, the Palmer had none: the Pilgrime trauailed to some
certaine place, the Palmer to all and not to any one: the Pilgrime might goe at his owne
charge, the Palmer must professe wilfull pouertie: the Pilgrime might giue ouer his profession,
the Palmer must be constant vntill he had obtained the Palme: that is, victorie ouer his ghostly
enemies, and life by death.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Full often time he had the bour begon abouen all nations in Pruce)</form> <xpln lang="en">That is, being of&shy;
ten among the Knights of the Dutch order, called <term lang="la">Ordo Teutonicus</term> in Prussia, he was for this wor&shy;
thinesse placed by them at the table, before any of what nation soeuer. If any desire to knowe the 
profession of these Knights called <term lang="la">Teutonici,</term> it was thus: They hauing their dwelling at Ierusalem 
were bound to entertaine Pilgrims, and at occasions to serue in warre against the Saracens. They 
were apparailed in white, and vppon their vppermost garment did weare a blacke crosse. And for 
that this order was first begun by a certaine rich Almaine, none were receiued into the same, saue 
onely gentlemen of the Dutch nation. After Ierusalem was last taken by the Saracens, <term lang="la">Anno 1184.</term>
these Knights retired to Tolemaida: and that being taken, into Germanie their owne countrey. 
And when as there also the people of Prussia vsed incursions vpon their confines, they went vn&shy;
to Fredericke the second then Emperor, <term lang="la">Ann. 1220.</term> who granted them leaue to make warres vpon 
them, and to turne the spoile to the maintenance of their order. After this conquest of Prussia, 
these knights grew rich, and builded there many Temples, and places of residence for Bishops, 
who also were enioyned to weare the habite of the Order. Chaucer will haue his Knight of such 
fame, that he was both knowne and honoured of this Order.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Alisandre)</form> <xpln lang="en"> <term lang="la">Alexandria</term> in Egypt, now called Scanderia.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Pruce)</form> <xpln lang="en"> <term lang="la">Prussia</term>, a countrey by Almaine and Russie.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Garnarde)</form> <xpln lang="en"> <term lang="la">Granata</term> in Spaine.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Algezer)</form> <xpln lang="en"> <term lang="la">Algezira</term>, a citie in Spaine neere the streights of Gilbralter.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Lettow)</form> <xpln lang="en"> <term lang="la">Lituania</term>, part of Sarmatia ioyning to Polonia.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Leyes)</form> <xpln lang="en">Taken to be <term lang="la">Levissa</term> vpon the continent not farre from Rhodes.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Belmari)</form> <xpln lang="en">Taken to be that countrey in Barbarie called by Vasseus, <term lang="la">Benamarin.</term></xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Great see)</form> <xpln lang="en"> <term lang="la">Mar maggione</term>: aunciently <term lang="la">Pontus Euxinus.</term></xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Satali)</form> <xpln lang="en">A citie in Anatolia, called sometime Atalia.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Tramissene)</form> <xpln lang="en">A citie in Barbarie in the Prouince, sometime called Mauritania Tingitana.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">Palathi)</form> <xpln lang="en"> <term lang="la">Palathia</term> in Anatolia.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">In all the orders fower is none, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">The foure orders of Fryers were these:
<lemeformat>
1 Friers Minors or gray fryers, Franciscanes.
2 Friers preachers or blacke friers, Dominicanes.
3 Friers Carmelites, or white friers.
4 Friers Augustines.</xpln></wordentry> 
</lemeformat>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">In eschange shildes sell)</form> <xpln lang="en">Shieldes in French called <term lang="fr">escus</term>, are French crownes, wherein this
Merchant did deale by returne.</xpln></wordentry> 

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">And gone to Vigils all before, and haue a mantill, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">It was the maner in times past, vpon 
festiuall Euens called <term lang="la">Vigili&aelig;</term>, for parishioners to meete in their Church-houses or Churchyards, 
and there to haue a drinking fit for the time. Here they vsed to end many quarels between neigh&shy;
bour and neighbour. Hither came the wiues in comely manner, and they which were of the bet&shy;
ter sort, had their mantles caried with them, as well for show, as to keepe them from cold at the 
table. These mantles also many did vse in the Church at morrow Masses and other times.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">For he was grounded in Astronomie)</form> <xpln lang="en">He that will be a Phisitian according to Homers prescri&shy;
</page>

<page sig="Bbbb3v">
ption. <term lang="gk"><damage></damage></term> that is, equiualent to any, ought to be skilfull in Astronomie and 
Magicke naturall. For if by Astronomie he be not able to iudge in what state the heauens stood, 
and what their Aspects were, when his patient sickned: And by Magick naturall to calculate his 
natiuitie, therebyto know which of the heauenly bodies ruled most in his birth, he shall hardly 
or but by chaunce coniecture to what end his sicknes will fort.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">In howers by his Magicke naturell &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">He meaneth this Phisitian was skilfull in Astro&shy;
logie, and could make his election of fortunate houres, wherein to minister his potions to his
patient: and likewise that he was practised in Magicke naturall, as in making of Sigills or Cara&shy;
cters stamped in mettall in their die times, fitted to that signe that gouerned that part of the bo&shy;
die, wherein the maladie was: as the stampe of <term lang="la">Aries</term> for the disease in the head, and of <term lang="la">Leo</term> for
the reynes, &amp;c. Hereof he speaketh in the third booke of Fame.
<lemeformat><term lang="me">And clerkes eke which conne well
All this Magicke naturell:
That craftily doe her entents
To maken in certaine ascendents
Images lo, through which Magick
They maken a man be whole or sick.</term>
</lemeformat>
</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="me">A Uernacle had he sowed vpon his cappe)</form> <xpln lang="en">A brooch or figure wherein was set the instru&shy;
ments wherewith Christ was crucified, and withall a napkin, wherein was the print of his face.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</section>

<section type="Annotations and Corrections: The Canterbury Tales">
<heading>
Annotations, with some Corrections.
</heading>

<wordgroup1 type="b" lang="en"><alpha>The Canterburie tales.</alpha>
<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">Fol. 1 Pag. 2</note> Campaneus,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Capaneus.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">2 2</note> Who shall giue a louer any law)</form> <xpln lang="en">Out of Boetius, <term lang="la">Lib. 3. Quis legem dat amantibus?
 Maior lex est sibi.</term></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">3 2</note> many,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Manie, that is, frensie.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">3 2</note> Nought comely like to louers maladie of Hereos)</form> <xpln lang="en"> Read <term lang="la">Eros</term>, i. Cupide; for so it
seemeth rather to be: which I gather thus. <term lang="la">Lucian</term> in his second Dialogue bringeth in
Cupid reaching Iupiter how to become amiable, and in him how louers may be made
acceptable to their Ladies; not by weeping, watching, and fasting, nor by furious me&shy;
lancholike fittes, but by comely behauiour. The words in the Greeke are thus much in 
Latine: <term lang="la">Si voles amabilis esse neque concutias &AElig;gida, neque fulmen geras: sed suavissimum te&shy;
ipsum exhibe: &amp; vestum sume purpuream, crepidas subliga auratas: ad tibiam &amp; ad Timpana
compositio gressu incede, &amp; vidibris qu&ograve;d plures te sequentur, quam Bacchum M&aelig;nades.</term> So that
the Louers of <term lang="la">Eros</term>, that is, Cupides seruants, doe carry themselues comely in all their 
passions; &amp; their maladies are such, as shew no open distemperature of bodie or mind:
which mediocritie this <term lang="la">Arcite</term> was farre from keeping.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">4 2</note> Full sooth is said that loue ne lordship, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">Out of Seneca: <term lang="la">Nec regna socium ferre, 
nec sed&aelig; solent.</term> And Ouid: <term lang="la">Non bene cum socijs regna Venusque manent.</term></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">5 1</note> Fie vpon a Lord that will haue no mercy, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">He blameth a prince that will rule 
by rigour, and not by discretion and gentlenes: as him in Homer. <term lang="gk"><damage> </damage></term> I am 
a king and bear a scepter, but I know not what is meet to be done. And in the 19. of the 
Iliads he giueth this precept to a king <term lang="gk"><damage></damage></term> Carrie a gentle heart 
within thee.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>

<page sig="Bbbb4r">
<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">Fol. 5 Pag. 1</note> Who may be a foole but if he loue, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">Seneca: <term lang="la">Amare &amp; sapere vix Deo conceditur.</term> Item, <term lang="la">Amans quid cupiat, scit, quid sapiat non videt.</term></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">5 2</note> That cost of gold largely a fother,&amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">A fother is twentie hundred weight, which
is a cart or a wayne load.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">6 2</note> Certres,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Starres</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">6 2</note> Puella and Rubeus)</form> <xpln lang="en">The names of two figures in Geomancie, representing two
constellations in heauen. <term lang="la">Puella</term> signifieth Mars retrograde, and <term lang="la">Rubeus</term> Mars direct.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">6 2</note> Atheon,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Act&aelig;on</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">6 2</note> Many a floreyne)</form> <xpln lang="en">A floreine is two shillings French, a Gilder is the same in Dutch.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">7 2</note> Unseriall,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Cerriall: Cerrus is a kind of tree like an Oke,and beareth maste. 
<term lang="la">vide Planium.</term></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">8 1</note> When Julianus had caught thee in his lans, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">Homer Odyss<emend err="">ey</emend> sheweth 
how Vulcane made a nette of yron where with he entrapped Mars and Venus: <term lang="gk"><damage> 
</damage></term> He compassed their bedde roun about with chaines or gynnes.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">8 2</note> While I dwell in the signe of the Lyon, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">For then is Saturne in his detriment, 
and in opposition to his owne house.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">9 1</note> Euery,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Either.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">9 2</note> <emend err="C">E</emend>xpulsed,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Expulsiue.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">10 1</note> Saue onely,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, And also.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">10 2</note> Armes straught,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, It Haught.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">11 .</note> For all forgotten is 
his vasselage,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, For Halfe forgotten is then 
his visage</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">12 1</note> That had men wed her similitude)</form> <xpln lang="en">According to the Greek Prouerbe, <term lang="gk"><damage></damage></term> 
For, <term lang="la">pari iugo dulcis tractus.</term> And Horace saith, <term lang="la">Disparem vites.</term> And Ouid counsailing the 
woman: <term lang="la">Siqua voles apt&egrave; nubere, nube pari.</term></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">13 1</note> Loth</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Lefe.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">13 1</note> Ye blessed be alway the leude man, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">To this purpose saith Aiax in Sophocles: 
<term lang="gk"><damage></damage></term> To know nothing is the best life.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">13 2</note> Curfew)</form> <xpln lang="en">William Conquerour in the first yeere of his raigne, commaunded that in
euery towne and village a bell should be rong euery night at eight of the clocke, &amp; that 
all people should then put forth their fire and candle and go to bed. The ringing of this
bell was called Curfew, that is, Couer fire.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">14 1</note> Knocked,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Coughed.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">14 2</note> On the beritote,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, On her dote.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">15 2</note> Sollar hall,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Schollars hall, or Vniuersitie hall, founded by the Chauncel&shy;
lour and Masters of the Vniuersitie, <term lang="la">Anno</term> 1326. but since vnited to the foundation of 
the Lady Elizabeth <term lang="la">de Burgo,</term> Countesse of Clare, and called Clare hall.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">17 2</note> Degrees was fortie, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">Here the Ascension of the Sunne is misprinted: For wher&shy;
as he saith 40 degrees, it must be 45. for then euerry body perpendicularly erected, hath 
the length of his shado.</xpln></wordentry>
</page>

<page sig="Bbbb4v">
<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">Fol. 19 Pag. 1</note> O infortunate ascendent Tortuous)</form> <xpln lang="en">Tortuous the signes are, which are called <term lang="la">ob&shy;
liqu&egrave; ascendentia:</term> that is, all from Capricorne to Cancer. So he calleth the <term lang="la">Ascendent</term> vn&shy;
fortunate, because it is one of those Signes, and had at the same time the Lorde of that 
Signe in his fall, which is in the Signe contrarie to his exaltation.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">21 2</note> There dronkennes raigneth in any rout, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">Horace. <term lang="la">Quid non ebrietas designat?
operta recludit.</term> Agreeing with HOmer Odyss. 14. <term lang="la">Vinum impellit bominem, vt nonnulla lo&shy;
quatur, qu&aelig;tacuisse pr&aelig;stisisset.</term></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">24 1</note> That Gawyn with his old curtesie, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">This Gawyn was sisters sonne to <term lang="la">Arthure</term>
the great king of the Brytaines, a most famous man in warre, and in all manner of ciuili&shy;
tie: As in the actes of the Britaines wee may reade. In the yeere 1082. in a prouince of
Wales called Rose was his sepulchre found, and his body affirmed by many to haue bin 
of the length of fourteene foot.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">25 1</note> Saue that Moles and king Salomon, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">Out of <term lang="la">Iosephus</term> and <term lang="la">Petrus Comestor, ma&shy;

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">25 2</note> Cherisheth blood natures frend)</form> <xpln lang="en">A precept giuen by most Phisitians. <term lang="la">Aristotle</term> in his 
2. booke <term lang="la">De partibus animalium</term> sheweth the reason why: <term lang="gk"><damage></damage></term> De&shy;
cay of blood (saith hee) bringeth corruption, for what is in wasting, easily suffereth de&shy;
triment either by cold or heat. And <term lang="la">Galen</term> saith, <term lang="la">Qui paucum habent sanguinem senium acce&shy;
lerare.</term></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">27 2</note> Theophrast is one of tho, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">In his booke <term lang="la">de frugalitate:</term> els in his treatise <term lang="la">De loqua&shy;
citate mulierum.</term></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">28 1</note> They connen so much craft in Wades bote, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">Concerning Wade and his bote

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">28 1</note> If thou wilt wisely wyrch loue thy wife)</form> <xpln lang="en">Agreeable to Homer Iliad. 9. <term lang="gk"><damage></damage></term> who so is a wise and sicrete man loueth and hath a care of his wife.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">35 2</note> He cleped it Ualerie and Theophrast)</form> <xpln lang="en">Some will haue vs read Valerie and his
Paraphrast. This Valerie wrote a book <term lang="en">De non ducenda vxore,</term> with a Paraphrase vpon it, 
which I haue seene in the studie of Master Allen of Oxford, a man of as rare learning 
as he is stored with rare bookes. His name was Gualterus Maape, Archdeacon of Ox&shy;
ford in the dayes of King Henry the second, but chaunged his name because he would 
not haue the Authour knowen, and termed it <term lang="la">Valerius ad Rusinum.</term> But yet there was one 
called Valerius, who wrote a booke of the same Argument printed among S. Ieromes 
workes. And likewise on called <term lang="la">Theophrastus Eresius,</term> who among many things did 
write of such matters. Let the Reader iudge.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">36 1</note> Of Pasiphae that was Queene of Crete, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">Wife of Minos King of Crete,
who hauing companie with a bull, but rather as Festus saith, with a man called Taurus:
brought forth Minotaurus, who was halfe a man and halfe a bull.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">38 1</note> Glad pouerte is an honest thing certain, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">Seneca <term lang="la">lib. 2. epist. Honestia res est lata 
paupertas.</term> This is not vnlike to that of Plutarch in his book <term lang="gk"><damage></damage></term> where hee

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">38 1</note> A great amender eke of sapience, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">Out of Theocritus, <term lang="gk"><damage></damage></term> Po&shy;
uertie etteth vp Artes, and is the mistresse of labour. And Seneca: <term lang="la">Melius in malis sa&shy;

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">38 2</note> Countrey,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Couentrie.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">39 1</note> Warriangles)</form> <xpln lang="en">Be a kind of birdes full of noyse and very rauenous, preying vpon
others, which when they haue taken, they vse to hang vpon a thorne or pricke, &amp; teare 
them in pieces and deuoure them. And the common opinion is, that the thorne wher&shy;
upon they thus fasten them and eate them, is afterwards poysonsome. In Staffordshire
and Shropshire the name is common.</xpln></wordentry>

and with vs it is called a Gods kichell, because godfathers and godmothers vsed com&shy;
monly to giue one of them to their godchildren, when they asked blessing.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">52 1</note> And such folly, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">To this purpose speaketh <term lang="la">Cornelius Tacitus</term> also of Mathematici&shy;

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">52 2</note> Collect, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">Collect,Expans, Yeares, &amp; Roots, are tearmes belonging to the Tolitane

nities belonging to the Planets.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">52 2</note> Alnath was shoue, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">Alnath is a fixed starre in the hornes of <term lang="la">Aries</term>, from whence
the first mansion of the moone taketh his name, and is called Alnath.</xpln></wordentry>

<term lang="me">Wherein he sought North and South 
And oft he sperred with his mouth 
In many a forrest wild

As farre as he had rid and gone

Till him there came a great Giant 
His name was cald Sir Oliphant 
A perrillous man of deed 
He swore, child by Termagaunt 
But if thou pricke out of my haunt 
Anon I slea thy steed 
Here is the Queen of Farry 
With Harpe and Pipe and Simphony 
Within this place and lure 
The child said also mote I thee 
To morrow will I meeten thee 
When I haue mine armour</term>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">84 1</note> Till fortune on the gallowes made him gape, &amp;c)</form> <xpln lang="en">This <term lang="la">Cr&oelig;sus</term> was the richest of all 
other, yet when hee asked <term lang="la">Solon</term> whether hee thought him the happiest of all other, <term lang="la">So&shy;
lon</term> answered, <term lang="gk"><damage></damage></term> I thinke thee rich indeede <term lang="la">Cr&oelig;sus</term>, and a King ouer

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">85 2</note> For when degrees 15 were ascended, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">Fifteene degrees of the equinoctiall rise 
euery equall houre: so that when 15 degrees were ascended in the Horizon after mid&shy;
night (for so he meaneth) then it is one of the clocke, about the which time is the first 
cocke, as they call it.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">87 1</note> Of Taurus was run forty degrees and one, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">This place is misprinted, as well

and hath in all but thirty degrees.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">87 2</note> I haue well read Dan Burnell the asse, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">Nigellus Wireker, Monke of Canter&shy;
bury, a man of great reading and iudgement, as Leland writeth of him,was not afraid to 
write of the faults of curates, &amp; the mispending of churchgoods euen to William Long&shy;
shamp Bishop of Ely and Lord Chancellor of England, a man of all men vnder the Sun
most malicious. He did write in verse to the foresaied William,a booke vnder the title of 
Brunellus, called <term lang="la">Speculum stultorum</term>: and this is it which Chaucer calleth here, Burnell
the asse. He liued <term lang="la">Anno</term> 1200. in the daies of king Iohn.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">88 2</note> I trow that yee haue droken wine Ape, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en"><term lang="la">Vinum Apianum:</term> that maketh one in
such taking, that he cannot with a straw hit a broad fanne: The cause is, for that after the 
drinking thereof vnmeasurably one thing seemeth two to the eyes: as saith <term lang="la">Iuuenall: Ge&shy;
minis exurgit mensa lucernis.</term> And Horace: <term lang="la">Saltat Milonius, vt semel icto accessit feruor captiti,
numerusque lucernis.</term></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">92 1</note> Rough,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Thus.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">95 1</note> Region,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Religion.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">97 1</note> Tenne at clocke it was, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">This must needs be two of the clocke: for that at tenne 
of the clocke the Sunne is in Ascension.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">97 1</note> I meane Libra)</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Taurus: Taurus is the moones exaltation.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">102 1</note> Cenes,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Sinnes.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">121 2</note> The lords son of Windsore,</form> <xpln lang="en">This may seeme strange both in respect that it is not in 
the French, as also in that there was no lord Winsore in those daies. But I take it thus: 
That although it stand not so in the French coppy, yet Chaucer vpon some conceipt did 
adde it, thereby to gratifie Iohn of Gaunt, or some other of the sonnes of Edward the 
third; who might well be called the lord of Windsore, not only for that hee was borne 
there, but also because at that time when as this booke was translated, the king had new&shy;
ly builded the castle of Windsore in such beautifull sort as could be deuised by that pro&shy;
uident and discreet surueyour William Wicham, and therefore was euery way the right 
lord of Windsore.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">146 2</note> Iustinian eke that made lawes, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">In the eleuenth booke of the <term lang="la">Code, De mendican&shy;
tibus validis:</term> where it is enacted, that if any shal begge hauing no cause either by need or 
maim, the same shalbe examined and searched: and who so shall find him to counterfer, 
and prooveth the same, <term lang="la">deminium eius consequetur:</term> and saith Bartol. He shall be punished 
<term lang="la">Ad arbitrium iudicis.</term></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">154 1</note> That he was well nigh wood &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">This madnesse counted by some an <term lang="la">indecorum</term> in 
such a valiant man, for as much as it proceeded of earnest loue, doth nothing disgrace 
<term lang="la">Troylus.</term> For <term lang="la">Socrates</term> in <term lang="la">Ph&aelig;dro Platonis</term>, praising loue, saith that it is not to be despised, 
because it is a kind of madnesse, and making there foure kindes of madnesse, hee proueth 
them all to be profitable. <term lang="gk"></term> <term lang="la">misterialem, poeticum, &amp; Amatorium.</term> With this last was 
<term lang="la">Troilus</term> possessed.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">171 1</note> At Dulcarnon)</form> <xpln lang="en">Dulcarnon is a proportion in Euclide, lib 1. <term lang="la">Theorem. 33. propos. 47.</term>
Which was found out by <term lang="la">Pythagoras</term> after an whole yeares study, and much beating of 
</page>

<page sig="Bbbb7r">
<note lang="en" type="m">Fol. Pag.</note> 
his brain. In thankfulnesse whereof he sacrificed an Oxe to the gods, which sacrifice he 
called Dulcarnon. Alexander Neckham an ancient writer in his book <term lang="la">Dei Naturis rerum</term>,
compoundeth this word of <term lang="la">Dulia</term>, and <term lang="la">Caro</term>, and will haue Dulcarnon to bee <term lang="la">quasi sacri&shy;
ficium carnis.</term> Chaucer aptly applieth it to Creseide in this place: shewing that shee was as 
much amazed how to answere Troylus, as Pythagorus was wearied to bring his desire
to effect.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">171 2</note> By ordall,&amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en"><term lang="la">Ordalia</term> is a triall of chastity by going through the fire, as did Emma
the mother of Edward the confessor: els ouer hote burning culters of yron barefooted,
as did Gunegond the wife of Henry the fifteene Emperor of Romains, as writeth Cran&shy;
tius in his Chronicles of Almaine.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">173 1</note> Entreat,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Encrease.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">195 1</note> But,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Bout.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">195 1</note> Philologi,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Phelgone.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">195 1</note> Undersmiling she was, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">So Homer often calleth Venus <term lang="gk"></term> and Theocritus 
<term lang="gk"></term> smiling or laughing Venus.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">196 1</note> Pane,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Way.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">204 1</note> Let him read Argonauticon)</form> <xpln lang="en">That is, let him read Appollonius Rhodius Alex&shy;
andrinus, who did write a booke so called,where he speaketh of Iason &amp; of them, which 
went with him to Colchis to fetch the golden fleece.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">205 2</note> Right as a wolfe, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">Read, sighs as when a Wolfe findeth a lambe alone.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">208 1</note> The Oule all night aboue the balkes wond, &amp;c.)</form> <xpln lang="en">Owles are accounted among 
those birds which are called <term lang="la">Aues sinistr&aelig;</term> because (as they write of them) they are foretel&shy;
lers of sorrow. <term lang="la">Ouid. F&aelig;daque fit volucris venturi nuncia luctus ignamus bubo, dirum mortabilius 
omen.</term> And <term lang="la">Virgil, Solaque culminibus ferals carmine bubo.</term></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">232 2</note> Dignitie,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Desteny.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">246 2</note> The Storke)</form> <xpln lang="en">This bird breedeth in the chimney tops of houses, and as it is written
of him, if the man or the wife commit adultery, he presently forsaketh the place. There&shy;
fore Chaucer calleth him the wreker, or rather the bewraier of adultery.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">247 2</note> Thou murderer of the Heisugge)</form> <xpln lang="en">The Heisugge called <term lang="la">Curruca</term>, is a little bird, in
whose nest, the Cuckow laieth her egs, and when they be hatched and growne to some
bignesse, they eat the bird that bred them.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">248 2</note> Frere,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Farre.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">254 2</note> Disdaine,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Distaine.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">272 1</note> Athalaus)</form> <xpln lang="en">read Athalanta who was daughter to C&aelig;neus, and contending in running
with them which did woe her, shee was at the last ouercome by Hippomenes, who cast
three golden apples in her way, which staied her in taking of them vp, and so shee was 
ouercome.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">279 1</note> Anticlaudianus)</form> <xpln lang="en">is a certaine booke written by one <term lang="la">Alanus de insulis. Gesner.</term></xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">330 1</note> To reken them all and also Rosamond)</form> <xpln lang="en">This Rosamond, the fair daughter of Wal&shy;
ter lord Clifford, was forced to be concubine to Henry the second, who builded for her 
at Woodstocke an house with a Labyrinth vnder the ground, much whereof at this day
</page>

<page sig="Bbbb7v">
<note lang="en" type="m">Fol. Pag.</note> 
is to be seene: as also a goodly Bath or well called to this day Rosamonds well. In the 
end she was poysoned by Queene Elianor, as some write, and being dead, was buried at 
God stow in an house of Nonnes beside Oxford: Not long since her graue was digged,
where some of her bones were found, and her teeth so white (as the dwellers there re&shy;
port) that the beholders did much wonder at them.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="en"><note lang="en" type="m">352 2</note> Make vp the verse thus:</form> <xpln lang="me">My name alas, my hart why makes thou strange.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">358 2</note> Corseint,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Forsent.</xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="g"><form lang="me"><note lang="en" type="m">360 1</note> Owe ne,</form> <xpln lang="en">read, Other then.</xpln></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>
</section>

<section type="Colophon">
These faults and many mo committed through the negligence of Adam 
Scriuener, notwithstanding Chaucers great charge to the contrary, might 
haue ben amended in the text it selfe, if time had serued: Whereas now no 
more, then the Prologues only, are in that sort corrected: which fell out so, be&shy;
cause they were last printed. Sentences also, which are many and excellent in 
this Poet, might haue ben noted in the margent with some marke, which now
must be left to the search of the Reader: of whom we craue Chaucers be&shy;
halfe that, which Chaucer in the end of one of his books requesteth for him&shy;
selfe,

<term lang="la">Qui legis, emendes autorem, non reprehendat.
FINIS</term>
</section>
</page>
